Pleiades Change Log: 2017-10-01 - 2017-10-31

New Place Resources

  • Amphithéâtre Gallo-Romain
    The ruins of the Roman amphitheater Saint-André-sur-Cailly are located in the hamlet of Bout Levé.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker
    Contributors:
  • Angkor
    The capital city of the Khmer empire, founded in the late 9th century by Yasovarman I (889-901). The city was sacked in 1431 by invaders from the Ayutthaya kingdom, and was abandoned except for the temple of Angkor Wat, which remained a Buddhist shrine.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors: Jeffrey Becker, Tom Elliott
  • Angkor Wat
    The principal temple of Angkor, the capital city of the Khmer empire, Angkor Wat was constructed in the 12th century CE. Angkor was inscribed as a UNESCO World Heritage site in 1992.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors: Jeffrey Becker, Tom Elliott
  • Bukhara
    Bukhara began as part of the Persian empire in the sixth century B.C. The site is especially important as an example of an Islamic city of central Asia. The city center was listed as a UNESCO World Heritage Site in 1993.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker
    Contributors:
  • Habuba Kabira (South)
    The southernmost of two associated settlements located on the western bank of the Euphrates, and one of the only examples of large-scale residential architecture from the Late Uruk period. Rescue excavations were conducted at the site before it was inundated by the Tabqa Dam Reservoir in the early 1970s.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors:
  • İşhan
    A village in Turkey, the site of a late antique Georgian church constructed in the 7th century.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors:
  • Ishkhani (monastery)
    A church and monastery in Tao-Klarjeti, first constructed in the 7th century.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors:
  • Kataret es-Samra
    An archaeological site containing Bronze Age burials located near the confluence of the Wadi Zarqa and the Jordan River.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors: Jeffrey Becker
  • Mesaoria
    A broad plain located in north-central Cyprus.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker
    Contributors:
  • Necropolis of Montessu
    The Necropolis of Montessu is located near Villaperuccio, Sardinia. It is the site of about 40 domus de Janas tombs and dates to the third millennium B.C.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker
    Contributors: Lindsay Holman
  • Panjakent
    The easternmost city of Sogdiana, inhabited from the fifth-ninth centuries CE.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors: Jeffrey Becker
  • Pantano di Grano necropolis
    A necropolis in the territory of Veii.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker
    Contributors:
  • Sardurihinili
    An Urartian fortress constructed in the eighth century BCE and destroyed in the seventh century BCE.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors: Jeffrey Becker
  • Tell el-'Oueili
    Tell el-'Oueili is an archaeological site located in the Dhi Qar Governorate of southern Iraq. Occupation at the site predates the occupation levels of Eridu, making Tell el-'Oueili one of the earliest known human settlements of southern Mesopotamia.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker
    Contributors:
  • Tell Habuba Kabira
    The northernmost of two associated settlements located on the western bank of the Euphrates, showing signs of habitation from the Late Uruk period through the Middle Bronze Age. Rescue excavations were conducted at the site before it was inundated by the Tabqa Dam Reservoir in the early 1970s.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors: Jeffrey Becker
  • Temple of Dionysos at Teos
    A Temple of Dionysos in the Ionic order begun in the Hellenistic period.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker
    Contributors:
  • Theater of Stratos
    The Theater of Stratos dates to the late fourth century B.C.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker
    Contributors: Tom Elliott
  • Toprak Kala
    The site of a walled Chorasmian city, inhabited from the first to sixth centuries CE, located approximately 40 kilometers north of the Afrighid capital city of Kath.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors: Jeffrey Becker
  • Walls of Timoleon
    The Greek fortifications of Gela that are credited to Timoleon of Corinth.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker
    Contributors:
  • Yiftahel
    An archaeological site of the Lower Galilee in northern Israel, Yiftahel has evidence for occupation beginning in the Pre-Pottery Neolithic B. By the early Bronze Age the settlement consisted of approximately 20 oval structures. Excavations took place here between 1992 and 2008.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker
    Contributors:

  • Agoranis (river)
    Arrian (quoting Megasthenes) lists the Agoranis among a large number of tributaries of the Ganges river. Some modern scholarship (e.g., Tomaschek in RE) has therefore identified it with the modern Gogra (Ghaghara); however, Erdosy in BAtlas silently rejects this view, preferring to identify it as a distributary of the Ganges, the modern Garai (Gorai) in Bangladesh.
    Creators: M.U. Erdosy
    Contributors: R. Talbert, Sean Gillies, Tom Elliott, Jeffrey Becker
    Modifications: replacing the abbreviation fl. with (river) to bring titles into conformance with editorial guidelines
    Actors: Tom Elliott

  • Allia (river)
    The Allia river was a tributary of the Tiber, but is now unlocated. It joined the Tiber some 18 km (11 miles) north of Rome and was most famous as the site of a battle in which the Romans were defeated by Brennus and the Gauls ca. 387 B.C.
    Creators: N. Purcell
    Contributors: R. Talbert, Sean Gillies, Tom Elliott, Jeffrey Becker
    Modifications: cleaned up extraneous whitespace in description. see https://github.com/isawnyu/pleiades-gazetteer/issues/140.

  • Angkor
    The capital city of the Khmer empire, founded in the late 9th century by Yasovarman I (889-901). The city was sacked in 1431 by invaders from the Ayutthaya kingdom, and was abandoned except for the temple of Angkor Wat, which remained a Buddhist shrine.
    Creators: Gabriel McKee
    Contributors: Jeffrey Becker, Tom Elliott
    Modifications: edited; initial revision; wikidata q163607
    Actors: Gabriel McKee, Jeffrey Becker

  • Centum Cellae

  • Eridu
    Eridu was an ancient city of Sumeria and perhaps one of the first true cities. The site is settled from ca. 5300 B.C. onward. Eridu was located approximately Located 12 km southwest of Ur and was the southernmost of a conglomeration of Sumerian cities that grew up around temple complexes.
    Creators: A. Hausleiter, M. Roaf, St J. Simpson, R. Wenke

  • Halaesa
    Halaesa is located on the north coast of Sicily between between Kalakta and Kephaloidion. The city was founded in 403 B.C. by Archonides of Herbita. The city sided with Rome during the First Punic War and was made "libera et immunis". By the Augustan period it had become a municipium.
    Creators: R.J.A. Wilson

  • Hypios (river)
    A river mentioned in ancient sources. Modern scholars identify it with the lower/larger (Büyük) course of the modern Melen Çay (Buyukmelen), which drains the marshy Efteni Golü (much diminished from its pre-modern extent). Ancient people seem to have extended the "Hypios" name past the lake and up one of its tributaries, but scholarly opinion is divided as to whether this was the modern Ugursuyu/Ogursu/Akarsu (Robert 1980, whence Foss in BAtlas) or the upper/smaller (Küçük) Melen Çayı (Strobel in New Pauly).
    Creators: C. Foss
    Contributors: R. Talbert, Sean Gillies, Tom Elliott, Jeffrey Becker
    Modifications: replacing the abbreviation fl. with (river) to bring titles into conformance with editorial guidelines
    Actors: Tom Elliott

  • Ilounon?
    An Early Roman hilltop oppidum, Ilunum was abandoned in the second century A.D. and then reoccupied in Late Antiquity (ca. A.D 590) as a Visigothic episcopal see during the Byzantine-Visigothic war. It was finally abandoned in the ninth century and the population was resettled in a new foundation, Murcia.

  • Lago dell’Accesa (archaeological site)
    The archaeological site of Lago dell’Accesa, in the locality known as "La Pesta", is situated nearby the lake of Lago dell'Accesa. The site was situated so as to exploit local mineral resources and the archaeological remains provide extensive evidence for habitation from the ninth through sixth centuries B.C.

  • Leontini
    A Greek colonial city founded by Naxians in 729 B.C., Leontini was reduced by Hippocrates of Gela in 498 B.C. Following the Sicilian Expedition, the city became subject to Syracuse. In Roman times it may have been of little importance and was destroyed by the Saracens in A.D. 848.
    Creators: R.J.A. Wilson

  • Ligustinus (lacus)
    The so-called Guadalquivir Marshes (Marismas del Guadalquivir) are a region of wetlands located on the lower Guadalquivir River. In antiquity these wetlands included a lagoon and an estuary known as Lacus Ligustinus. Silting gradually transformed the lake into wetlands.
    Creators: Jr., F.H. Stanley, R.C. Knapp

  • Magidû
    Magidû or Megiddo (modern Tell al-Mutesellim, "The Tell of the Governor") is an ancient settlement mound located in northern Israel near Kibbutz Megiddo, about 30 km southeast of Haifa. Bronze Age Megiddo was an important Canaanite city-state and during the Iron Age, it was a royal city in the Kingdom of Israel. The site is mentioned in Neo-Assyrian sources.
    Creators: Claudia Horst
    Contributors: Jeffrey Becker, Jamie Novotny, Tom Elliott
    Modifications: cleaned up extraneous whitespace in description. see https://github.com/isawnyu/pleiades-gazetteer/issues/140.

  • Marmenia
    The Domus Marmeniae is a villa located on the Via Appia near the fourth milestone. The villa grounds hosted a pagan necropolis and tradition holds that the matrona, Marmenia, relocated the body of St. Urban to a mausoleum built on the grounds.

  • Nineveh/Ninos
    This important Mesopotamian city flanks the eastern edge of the Tigris flood plain, opposite modern Mosul, of which it is now a suburb. From the third millennium B.C. onwards, Nineveh was the most important religious center of the goddess Ištar in the area that would become the Assyrian heartland. Starting in the Middle Assyrian period, the city came under the authority of the kings of Assyria, who often sponsored large-scale building activities there. However, it was not until 704 B.C. that Nineveh became the administrative capital of Assyria, when the Neo-Assyrian king Sennacherib moved the royal family and court there and transformed the city into a thriving imperial metropolis. Nineveh remained Assyria’s capital until 612 B.C., when it was captured and destroyed by a Babylonian-Median collation led by Nabopolassar and Cyaxares. The visible remains of the (7th-century) Assyrian city include the citadel mound Kuyunjik, the smaller mound of Nebi Yunus, and the twelve-kilometer-long city wall.
    Creators: M. Roaf, T. Sinclair, S.E. Kroll, St J. Simpson

  • Santi Giovanni e Paolo
    An ancient Christian basilica on the Caelian Hill in Rome that was built in AD 398 by will of a senator known as Pammachius. The basilica was located over the home of two Roman soldiers, John and Paul, who had been martyred under Julian in AD 362. The church was thus called the Titulus Pammachii.
    Creators: Jeffrey Becker

  • Uley
    At West Hill, Uley, Gloucestershire, excavations revealed the remains of a multi-period ritual site. This site remained in use from the Neolithic period to the Middle Ages. An Iron Age sanctuary was replaced by a Romano-British temple dedicated to Mercury in the early second century A.D.
    Creators: A.S. Esmonde Cleary
    Contributors: DARMC, R. Talbert, Scott Vanderbilt, R. Warner, Jeffrey Becker, Sean Gillies, Tom Elliott

  • Umma
    Umma was an important ancient city of Sumer but has not been scientifically excavated. The mainstream of scholarly opinion associates the ancient site with Tell Jokha, Iraq, located in Iraq's Northwestern Dhi Qar Governorate. This area is remote and is associated with a network of ancient canals. Tell Jokha measures about 1,500 meters in diameter.
    Creators: B. Siewert-Mayer, W. Röllig, H. Kopp
    Contributors: Sean Gillies, Francis Deblauwe, Jeffrey Becker, Jamie Novotny, Ryan M. Horne, Eric Kansa
    Modifications: edited; reference; references

  • Vapheio Tholos Tomb
    The Vapheio Tholos Tomb is a Mycenaean tholos built around 1500 BC that is assumed to have held an elite burial or burials. It is best known for the two golden Minoan-style cups it housed.
    Creators: Michael Horst
    Contributors: Elise Johnson, Brady Kiesling, Sean Gillies, Michael Horst, Jeffrey Becker, Emily van Zanten, Adam Rabinowitz, Tom Elliott, Dalton Parish
    Modifications: baseline created; cleaned up extraneous whitespace in description. see https://github.com/isawnyu/pleiades-gazetteer/issues/140.
    Actors: Tom Elliott, Tom Elliott
  • Velitrae
    Velitrae (modern Velletri) was an ancient Volscian settlement. In the Roman Republican period, Velitrae was attacked by Marcus Furius Camillus (379 B.C.) and again by Cincinnatus (377 B.C.); the Romans controlled the site by 365 B.C. The gens Octavia hailed from the city and a youthful Octavian spent time there.
    Creators: L. Quilici, S. Quilici Gigli
    Contributors: DARMC, R. Talbert, Brady Kiesling, Sean Gillies, Johan Åhlfeldt, Jeffrey Becker, Tom Elliott
    Modifications: darmc 18994
    Actors: Jeffrey Becker

  • Via Appia
    An early Roman road (via publica) originating at Rome and terminating at Brundisium, the Via Appia was begun in the fourth century B.C. by the censor Appius Claudius Caecus. The Latin author Statius described the Via Appia as "queen of the long roads".
    Creators: L. Quilici and S. Quilici Gigli
    Contributors: R. Talbert, Sean Gillies, Tom Elliott, Jeffrey Becker
    Modifications: references
    Actors: Jeffrey Becker
